193700788X|1.0|What a disappointing end to a series I used to love, and used to be obsessed with talking about. Sloppy writing, mediocre plots with no sense of suspense, characters acting out of character, and sudden contradictions of cannon created by the writer herself, made for this to be a sad ending.<br /><br />The two murder plots were embarrassingly amateurish and full of plot holes, complete with impromptu kidnappings in front of an entire bar full of people (because that was always going to work) and 11th hour introductions to devils and soulless humans (um...what? Since when?).  And even when people are held at gunpoint (yet again), there was no sense of suspense. I just waited for Sookie to show up, get shot (again), with someone there to immediately rescue her (again). And while it may have been sweet to bring back all the characters for a final curtain call, each with one miniscule piece to solve the puzzle, it made for a tedious, jumbled mess of a plot. It would have been better to split up the last scenes with every character over two books, at least, so it wouldn't feel so contrived.<br /><br />This book also included for the first time not only Sookie's 1st person POV, but also a 3rd person POV. While this helped with telling parts of the story that occurred away from Sookie, it was jarring to go from 1st person POV to 3rd POV and back again. Harris would have been better served to follow Rachel Caine's example in her Morganville series when she simply added other 1st person POVs in order to give the reader more information.<br /><br />As for the characters acting out of character, it didn't feel like a single major character acted true to form, except for perhaps Pam. We have Eric acting like a desperate a-hole, claiming he had always wanted to turn Sookie, even against her will, even when in previous books he had promised not to do so (and he's always come through on his promises, whether you like him as a character or not). We have Bill acting surprisingly immature and douche-baggy when he basically says, \\"Nyah, nyah, nyah, Eric. I'm telling on you!\\" We have Sam now claiming he's always loved Sookie, but was prevented from pursuing her by Eric. However, Sam has had 12 books to make a move on Sookie, not including all the years Sookie had worked for him before DUD even started. And other than a minor spark of interest that fizzled quickly in DUD when Bill showed his interest first (as though Sam finally realized Sookie was a girl), and a spontaneous make-out session in DAAD (which Sookie quickly decides was a mistake), he hasn't shown any interest in her at all. And in fact, seems to have a bad habit of falling for women who want Sookie dead (Callista, Tanya, and Jannalyn). And now we're supposed to believe he didn't think he was good enough for Sookie because he had something against his shifter background? I might have believed that before he turned into a fricking LION in FDTW, but not after. And finally we have Sookie, who was always a champion for humans and supes alike to be treated equally, now says nothing when Tara spouts off bigotry against the supes, and even starts thinking like her. We have Sookie who realizes the cluviel dor has affected her feelings and way of thinking, and decides it's nothing, when she had such huge issues dealing with the blood bond (which did NOT, in fact, manipulate her feelings after all). Apparently, the cost of using the cluviel dor is that Sookie loses her ability to think for herself.<br /><br />If all this weren't confusing at best, and incredibly frustrating at worst, there are the numerous contradictions of cannon in this book. There is the obvious one where Sookie cannot read shifter thoughts, but only feelings, when of course she's shown that she can read the specific thoughts of several weres (including Sam himself, more than once) in several books. There is the 11th hour reveal that the maenad's call is irresistible to weres - and Sam only brought this up now because....? Asmentioned above, there is also 11th hour introduction of devils and soulless humans, which of course is largely unexplained and poorly constructed. Fairy is supposedly closed, for good this time by Niall, and yet Claude - who has proved himself to be rather dimwitted - manages to break out of not only fairy prison (which Niall admitted to allowing) but Fairy itself? What, is the door to Fairy closed with tape?<br /><br />Finally, the HEA that Harris promised us was not actually delivered, simply a vague assumption by Sookie that there would be an HEA further down the line. However, she didn't sound all that excited about it, and even admitted that if her HEA walked away that very moment that she would be fine with it. Boy, if that isn't romantic....wait, it's not. I knew that the HEA would either be Eric or Sam. I saw the very minor hints pointing to Sam, but dismissed them as red herrings. Sookie, up until the end of the last book, was by her own words in love with Eric (and if we can't trust her words when the entire story is told from her POV, who can we trust?). While she admired Sam's physique from time to time, there was no mention of actual physical or emotional attraction like there was for Eric. I also dismissed the hints, especially after the last book, because in order to make Sam work I knew Sookie would have to break things off with Eric irrevocably (or he would continue to pursue her as he's done from practically the beginning of the series), get over her feelings for Eric (that are well-documented), realize she has feelings for Sam now beyond that of friendship (when she hasn't in the past), determine Sam has feelings for her as well (when HE hasn't in the past), and then have the passionate HEA that everyone was hoping for. All in one book. Considering how long Harris spent building up the relationship between Eric and Sookie in the other books, through her relationships with Bill and Quinn and her attraction to Alcide, this did not seem as though it could be done. At least, there was no way to do this without a deus ex machina, but then Sookie's reactions would not be believable since she has such issues with the blood bond. I had no idea how things could work out between Eric and Sookie considering the huge obstacles before them, but I also couldn't conceive of a way Harris could make a Sam ending believable either. I thought Sookie ending up alone but content would be the most believable ending, but since Harris promised us an HEA, that was out as well. So I was confused, and worried that the ending would be a poorly written one that wouldn't really satisfy anyone. I was wrong. It was so much worse.<br /><br />So, we have mystery that's not mysterious, action and suspense that are dull and predictable, and romance that falls flat. It feels like Harris lost her inspiration for the series and the characters, for whatever reason, but had to push on anyway due to signed contracts. Very depressing all the way around. I simply cannot recommend this book.mentioned above, there is also 11th hour introduction of devils and soulless humans, which of course is largely unexplained and poorly constructed. B00BWJARHO|1.0|*** This review may contain spoilers ***<br /><br />I hated pretty much everything about this movie, but here are the main points:<br /><br />Expanding on Tolkien's mythology: Presumably, the reason you should \\"never trust an Elf!\\" (Gimli's line in TFotR) is because Elves turned their back on the Dwarfs when they were being attacked by Smaug? What were they expected to do against a dragon and why were they just standing there anyway? This is basically adding a whole new level of mythology. A no-no, unless it's an improvement and that's not the case here.<br /><br />The dwarfs: They looked ridiculous to me and their behavior was annoying. But no dwarf was more off-putting than Thorin with his ludicrous fur-trimmed leather duster, Rob Zombie do and constant glowering. Ugh.<br /><br />The introductory scene takes too long but, given all this time, you'd think Jackson would try and be more faithful to the book. But, no, he still manages to mangle it. Anyway, the next day, after Bilbo has decided not to go the day before (he wasn't really given the choice in the book), he just...goes. And it's very interesting that he's able to, on foot, overtake Gandalf and the dwarfs all riding along briskly on horseback. And when he does catch up, he's offered a pony, which he at first declines. Why? Bilbo had nothing against riding a pony.<br /><br />Azog: The footnote springs to cinematic life and has a vendetta against Thorin. The character in the LotR Appendix, for all of his two-pages of appearance and death at the hands of Dáin, is still more bad-ass than the video game reject in AUJ. Anyway, if Jackson wanted to go this route, it would have made more sense to just go with Azog's son Bolg, who will appear anyway.<br /><br />Trolls: or, how to take an amusing sequence from the book and drain it of any humor whatsoever. Actually, that opinion that applies to the whole movie. But, instead of Gandalf employing ventriloquism on the trolls and getting them to fight each other, Bilbotells them the dwarfs have parasites and Gandalf gets to break a rock in two. Yawn.<br /><br />Radagast: An utterly ridiculous and repellent visualization of another footnote character, with his ridiculous dying hedgehog, rabbit-drawn sleigh and poo-smeared face. But that's just my opinion. But why is he in the movie at all? To tell Gandalf about the Necromancer, I guess. But if Gandalf doesn't already know this (as he does in the book) where did he get the map and key from Thráin (Thorin's father) who (in the book) was a prisoner in Dol Guldur? Presumably, he wasn't in Dol Guldur in the movie. So where is he? It's basically glossed over. In any case, the very idea that Greenwood is turning into Mirkwood right before Radagast's eyes at the start of the movie just strikes me as a desperate and weak attempt to create a sense of urgency and menace. The spiders are even seen entering the forest as if to take up their positions. Also in any case, Gandalf's very reason for being in Middle Earth is to help combat the Necromancer (aka Sauron). For him not to know about his presence in Mirkwood is, sorry, just witless.<br /><br />Apparent invulnerability: None of the protagonists get hurt in this movie. Stone giants fall on them, they get up. They fall a hundred feet, they just walk it off. Goblins (who, for some reason, are portrayed as distinct from orcs) attack in droves, they just cut them down like wheat. Thorin does later fall unconscious (from a blow that should have crushed his skull), but he later turns out none the worse for wear. Even if these characters weren't profoundly irritating, why should I care about them if they're not in any danger? And enough with the fake deaths. Did anyone watching this really fear the titular character might fall to his death halfway through his own movie?<br /><br />Riddles in the \\"Dark\\": Finally, some resemblance to the book, except even here there are details changed for no good reason. And couldn't they have toned down the lighting just a bit more to give some impression of darkness? As for Gollum himself, it's hard to find fault with Andy Serkis, except that Gollum is now basically a celebrity and practically played like one in the film. He no longer surprises, frightens or disturbs in the least. The cosy lighting doesn't help at all.<br /><br />Bilbo the Badass: Bilbo kills a Warg (which, though strong enough to uproot a tree, will drop with a single arrow or sword thrust/hack). Later he savagely kills an orc. Where'd this come from? Perhaps to make up for Frodo being a wuss in those other movies? Whatever the case, it's so far out of left field, it's just seems tacked on.<br /><br />Eagles: Gandalf just happens to spot a moth nearby in the tree he's climbed up to avoid the orcs and wargs. How convenient. And the book's explanation for the eagles doesn't work because...? There really is no good reason. Jackson wants to make full use of the eagles, but only as devices; he's averse to giving them any back story or personality or anything. You'd think with his apparent love of exposition and the characters making speeches all the time, something could have been said about them, but no, the eagles just drop them off and depart. They're nothing more than deus ex machina. Granted, there are challenges to following the book more closely here but I still think Jackson takes the cheap way out.<br /><br />Final scene: Bilbo blithely states \\"I do believe the worst is behind us.\\" Really? Why would he believe this while looking at a mountain that is 1) still leagues distant; and 2) he knows contains a fire-breathing dragon?<br /><br />I will be surprised if even morbid curiosity compels me to watch any more of these.

0393345084|5.0|&#34;End This Depression Now&#34; is an easy-to-read explanation of what ails the U.S. economy. Krugman is a professor of economics at Princeton, a Nobel Prize winner, and a columnist for the New York Times. He's also a liberal with a political agenda. Krugman has that rare gift of making complex macroeconomic subjects understandable to non-economists. Krugman is also worth reading because his predictions over the last five years have proved eerily prescient.<br /><br />Krugman is a Keynesian who argues that insufficient private spending is the cause of the America's current economic problems. The initial root cause was the collapse of the housing bubble. Falling house prices reduced demand in the economy. Jobs have been lost in construction but people are poorer because their wealth has fallen and therefore they are spending less money. Krugman argues that the U.S. was in a similar position after the 1929 stock market crash. He argues that only governments can stimulate demand on the scale needed. Krugman argues that the Obama stimulus plan wasn't ambitious enough.<br /><br />Republicans have favored balancing the budget and cutting taxes. Krugman argues that this is what Herbert Hoover tried to do in the early 1930s and the result was a 25% unemployment rate. Krugman claims that the economic malaise in the West is similar to the depression of the 1930s and suggests that we should adopt Keynesian economic policies. He states that we are in a depression and our problems won't be solved by quantitative easing alone. Krugman believes it is a problem of demand (or a lack of spending to put it another way). One man's spending is somebody else's income. The Western economies are just not spending enough. Krugman is particularly scathing about the austerity measures in Europe, which he says are a mistake and are clearly not working.<br /><br />Krugman argues that there is less spending because of high unemployment and low confidence. &#34;Printing money&#34; the Friedmanite solution really hasn't worked in this recession because the fall in demand has been so severe. Given that the private sector is unable to respond, Krugman argues that the government must increase spending.<br /><br />Krugman bases his argument on what happened in the 1930s. The initial government response to the Great Depression was ineffective, as Hoover insisted that the economy was sound and that prosperity would soon return. Hoover believed he must restore public confidence so businesses would begin to invest and expand production. This would provide jobs and income and restore the economy to health. However, business owners saw no reason to increase production while unsold goods clogged their shelves. By 1932 investment had dropped to less than 5 percent of its 1929 level.<br /><br />Convinced that a balanced federal budget was essential to restoring business confidence, Hoover sought to cut government spending.  But in the face of a collapsing economy, this served only to reduce demand further. As conditions worsened, Hoover's administration eventually provided emergency loans to banks and industry, expanded public works, and helped states offer relief. But it was too little, too late.<br /><br />By 1939, the U.S. unemployment rate was still high at 11% (it is 7.5% at the moment). Roosevelt was reluctant to accept any more deficit spending than seemed absolutely necessary to prevent mass suffering. As a result, he did not create an unbalanced budget on the scale Keynes advocated until World War II forced it upon him. Once the government started spending at the levels Keynes had suggested, the depression ended.<br /><br />The view amongst many on the right is that government borrowing is too high. However, the U.S. economy grew in the 1940s, funded by borrowing. U.S. government spending as a percentage of GDP was significantly higher in 1945 than it is today.  However, the U.S. was able to create jobs for returning serviceman and pay for the Marshall Plan. 0307596885|4.0|Sometimes less is more and such is the case with the fourteen short stories that make up DEAR LIFE by Alice Munro, a skilled wordsmith whose pared-down yet elegant prose leaves you with just enough of a story to form your own novel making this reader feel a participant in the telling of the tale itself.<br /><br />\\"In story after story, she illumines the moment a life is forever altered by a chance encounter or action not taken, or by a simple twist of fate that turns a person out of his or her accustomed path and into a new way of being or thinking.\\"<br /><br />In each story, as is true in life, one moment can change everything.  In Amundsen, one of the very best of the fourteen stories, bride-to-be changes into her wedding dress to find the groom has made changes of his own.  In Gravel, parents make a selfish choice and the life of a child is forever changed.  And in Corrie, a moment of clarity opens the eyes of a lover.  My favorite story of the lot is In Sight of the Lake about a forgetful woman trying to find her way only to realize that she really isn't going anywhere.<br /><br />The last four stories of the book are, according to the author, \\"autobiographical in feeling, though not, sometimes, entirely so in fact\\".  These stories are told with a certain zest that I didn't find in the first ten stories of fiction.  Perhaps Ms. Munro's true life stories told with a twinge of elaboration are the biggest delight of this book.<br /><br />This writer has left me very curious.  This is the first of her work, though definitely not the last, that I've had the privilege of reading.  Welcome to my dear literary life Ms. Munro.
B00BQ1DBUS|5.0|Even though he's been musically active for the past two decades,Rod's activity during the new millennium so far has been as purely an interpretive singer. During this time,interestingly enough I began to become more interested in who Rod Stewart was in the earliest days of his solo career. It was then my privilege to be presented with an artist who was both vocally and songwriting wise up to the challenge to bridge any gaps that may have existed between soul,blues,folk,country and rock n roll during the early/mid 1970's. His instrumental and vocal sound was not only unique and distinct but was also quite a storyteller as well. He walked the line between rock star and singer/songwriter troubadour to perfection. Over the years,he became exactly the opposite of that,and seemed to contentedly slip into near has been status:churning out one standards/covers album after the other. Apparently he was content with his status: he was merely filling in time because his songwriting muse had temporarily abandoned him. Well after writing his autobiography,everything changed for him and hiss muse returned. And this album is an expression of that.<br /><br />  \\"She Makes Me Happy\\",\\"Can't Stop Me Now\\" and \\"Beautiful Morning\\" are all melodically powerful uptempo numbers,with a strong dance/rock rhythm and that collision of fiddle and acoustic guitar that typified his signature instrumental sound. \\"It's Over\\" finds him in the same instrumental frame of mind of a beautifully romantic ballad. \\"Brighten Beach\\" is musically in the middle ground,but is a lyrically beautiful and touching tale of reflection on teenage romance with age and is filled with allusions to Martin Luther King Jr,Ramblin' Jack Elliot and others who personally influenced Rod in the past. \\"Live The Life\\" is a pop/rock song with a stronger,slower groove to it,with Rod advising his collage age son not to neglect living it up with a loved one over pure academia. The title song is actually my favorite on the album-a beautiful combination of folk/soul and gospel based harmony and a poetic reflection on lost love. \\"Picture In A Frame\\" is a similarly beautiful organ fueled soul ballad as well that Rod sings as...well only he can sing it. \\"Sexual Religion\\" is a modern day variation of his funky dance/rock sound of the late 70's with a treatise on modern day women in the intimately dominant roles once stereotypically male. \\"Make Love To Me Tonight\\" and \\"Pure Love\\" lead off the album on two epic yet swooning romantic notes.<br /><br />  After all this time away from the songwriting game,one might think Rod Stewart's ability to craft songs that possessed the musical and emotional wallop of his early solo career might not be possible. The interesting thing about this album is that production wise it owes its attitude to the productions of his late 80's/early 90's albums to a certain degree. Yet in terms of instrumentation and the writing itself this album actually blasts right back to his peak years of [[ASIN:B00000612O Gasoline Alley]] and [[ASIN:B00000612P Every Picture Tells a Story]]-almost as if some of the unevenness that dogged some of his later albums never happened at all. As Rod Stewart enters elderhood, there are more than a few indications here that he is coming to reconcile his past-both personally and musically. Not only does this make good fodder for a songwriter returning from a long period of writers block,but provides an important flavor to the lyrical quality about this album. These songs are full of little words of wisdom,and nostalgia that somehow never comes across as cheap and insincere. It's the man behind \\"Maggie May\\" and \\"Tonight's The Night\\",only with more miles on him and looking at life closer to the final round of the marathon of living. On everyone of these levels: musically,lyrically and thematically this album is an all time winner for him. Some have correctly stated that this thing isn't jam-packed with recipes, but of course, it wasn't intended as a collection of bread recipes. It is exactly what it says it is: &#34;The Fundamentals of Artisan Bread and Pizza.&#34; Do you want to understand more about any or every aspect of breadmaking beyond what the average home baker knows (&#34;let rise 1 or 2 hours until doubled; punch down and place in bread pan. Cover and let rise another hour or so until just above the edge of the pan&#34;)? This is a great book for helping you understand what yeast is, how it works, how flavors develop, how protein levels affect the end product, how temperature and time are ingredients (not restrictions!!), and several other tidbits regarding how professional breadmakers tend to think about their breads.<br /><br />Think of this book as a self-contained course in artisan breadmaking. Bite off a little at a time, until you've gone through the whole book! Set aside ~$125 for &#34;course materials&#34; (dutch oven, kitchen scale, large container, and maybe a brotform or two), and you'll come to appreciate what a fine job Mr. Forkish has done in producing this book. I recommend it wholeheartedly.

B00CI9F786|4.0|As a long-time fan of Paolo Coelho's works, I was eager to read \\"Manuscript Found in Accra\\". In his latest, Coelho channels spiritual enlightenment by means of an intriguing premise. The discovery of a manuscript dating back to 1099 serves as the medium through which a mysterious figure known as the Copt expounds on questions pertaining to life. The advice given by this sage veers between the banal and the profound and it appears some of the sayings are adapted from holy books such as the Bible and also the Koran.<br /><br />Although I enjoyed the work and the timely reminders of some of these sayings and advice, I admit I was a tad disappointed as this book did not quite seem like a novel to me but more of a manual for living life, as seen in some of Coelho's earlier works such as [[ASIN:0060527986 Warrior of the Light: A Manual]]. Unlike some of my favorite Coelho works such as [[ASIN:0061122092 By the River Piedra I Sat Down and Wept: A Novel of Forgiveness]], and [[ASIN:0061124265 Veronika Decides to Die: A Novel of Redemption]], I did not get the sense of an actual plot but more like a series of conversations such as those found in [[ASIN:0691097186 The Collected Dialogues of Plato: Including the Letters (Bollingen Series LXXI)]].<br /><br />I have mixed feelings about this work, hence the 3.5*. On the one hand, it is not quite what I expected in a Coelho novel, but on the other hand, I found myself unexpectedly finding gems I wasn't particularly looking for within these pages, lending credence to the scriptural saying, \\"Seek and you will find.\\"

The failure of the government to even meet the needs of its own elite and the collapse of its society, the willingness to do anything to say in power, to keep up the idea that everything it going well. This is not just lists of government numbers and data, all crisp and washed and presented for the public's pleasure or us to shake our heads over.  We get their loves, their failings, their day by day survive to keep themselves and their families fed, free, and human.  We watch them escape, return, arrested, abused, and escape again. She was so happy to be able to interact with somebody without being overwhelmed with their thoughts, that she was unable to judge them or to keep from going overboard.  For a long time, she saw all of the new kinds of people she met as being just like her, only easier for her to be with.  Through the books she gradually found that the vampires were very political and difficult to understand or trust (Bill, of course, began their whole relationship because he was ordered by his master, and Eric, who she loved and continually forgave, routinely tricked her into doing things she did not want to do--like marry him, or create a blood bond with him).<br /><br />She learned that she loved her life, being in the sun, going to church, working and keeping her house.  She learned that there were some traditions that she would never be able to accept, such as the \\"open marriages\\" with the weres, where Alcide and Calvin were responsible for impregnating numerous women, or being turned to a vampire.<br /><br />I thought that the idea that Eric wanted her to turn has been coming for several books, but in this book she finally accepts that Eric would turn her without her consent.  It's odd that this would be a surprise since he was always doing things without her consent that he thought were in her best interest, but I thought it was compelling to have her look at Eric's other two \\"children\\" and realize that she was just part of a matching set.  After all, they had been Eric's lovers at first, and now where more friends/allies than wives.  She has also been thinking about children periodically for several books, also a tough thing for her to give up.<br /><br />She and Sam have had a sparky relationship for a long time.  She has  been holding back because she is afraid to loose him as a friend, but also in the youthful feeling that there is something else out there---something better---something more exciting. I think the outcome was forecast in the last book---If she did not love Sam, the magic would not have worked and she would not have been able to bring him back from the dead. And I think Eric knew this too.<br /><br />I think that the Sookie in this book has become a strong mature woman.  She knows the kind of life she wants, and she is willing to admit it to herself.  She wants to be a part of her home town, to garden, to help her friends, and to raise a family.  She no longer lusts after adventure, or those who are connected with it.  She is no longer isolated, and has earned the friendships she has as well as the acceptance of the people in town.  I don't think it is an accident that when she is kidnapped toward the end of the book, that she is rescued, not by supernatural beings, but by humans that want to help her.<br /><br />Sam matches what she really wants out of life.  She has tried other things, experimented with other life styles, and ultimately knows what will make her happy.  I'm glad she has the confidence to go with what she wants.<br /><br />(Although, I was hoping she would find out that her fairy blood gave her immortality and she could be with Eric forever. :-). Still can't complain too much as I believe the book does make sense and Sookie seems to be happy)

B008AK8FHM|4.0|While I have to disagree with the title Proof of Heaven that this book was actually not proof of heaven, I found the premise and the experience shared in this book quite fascinating. Dr. Alexander talks about his experiences while in a coma from a serious bacterial meningitis attack that is quite rare and shuts down the neo-cortex of the brain, the part that makes us, basically, human, and puts him in a coma he was not expected to live through or return from. He shares his vivid memories of experiences he had in a place outside of this realm of existence, memories of acceptance, love, inclusiveness, connectedness, peace, calm and so many good things. The main point that of love, unconditional and overwhelming, in a way that isn't even able to be expressed on this world.<br /><br />Dr. Alexander considers this absolute proof of God and Heaven, where I see it as proof that there is something after this life, but not necessarily the religious versions of God and Heaven as we have been told in the Bible or through other religious upbringings. I find the concept of unconditional love fascinating, and somewhat comforting, and I definitely like the perspective of life of some sort, existence and consciousness of some sort, after this body fades away, especially now that I have been diagnosed with a terminal illness, and thinking about death and dying is something that has come to my mind sooner than it would for most people.<br /><br />I enjoyed his personal stories about his journey. I appreciated his honesty about how he was not really a believer prior to this experience, that he was a staunch man of science, to a point, and how this changed that. I like the way he now weaves these two things--the science and the faith--together to be a better doctor and a better person.<br /><br />I like that he provides other resources and information for the reader to read, to consider, and to decide for themselves what they think happened. I went to many of his other resources and read them and even saw him in some video where he was interviewed about his experiences. It's all very compelling... and again, somewhat comforting, especially to me. See, I have a terminal illness, and I know my death is more imminent than I had original expected it to be... life and death, this life and the afterlife, have both been big on my mind. This book did provide me some comfort, some feeling that there might be something else out there beyond this. I think I needed that. At the same time, there was some compelling stories that tied some things in his life together in a way that seems only able to happen outside of this realm. You'll have to read his story to learn more about it. If you're interested, but not sure about buying the book, look him up online and read some of the places where he talks about near death experiences with others who have had them too. Fascinating stuff, and it might help you make up your mind if this is something you want to consider buying or not. I consider it money well spent, if for nothing but the fascination factor.<br /><br />If you're not a believer, this book might make you think about an afterlife differently. The \\"fight or flight response\\" is our body's primitive, automatic, inborn response that prepares the body to \\"fight\\" or \\"flee\\" from perceived attack, harm or threat to our survival.<br />There are  experiences that unfortunately happen in the lives of the young  that may cause (force) one into adult situations and adult thinking.  When faced with life threatening situations,  the mind stops acting like a child and starts protecting like an adult,  and the ones around you that witness what you are going through find a way to cope in their own way and try to protect you the only ways they know how, even though they don't always comprehend what is actually happening. 1439190275|1.0|A year ago, I would've given this book 5+ stars. I read this book along with 'Good Calories, Bad Calories', 'How I Gave Up my Low Fat Diet and Lost 40 Pounds\\", \\"Wheat Belly\\" and many many more.<br /><br />I started the diet in September of 2011 and lost 10 pounds the first month. I was thrilled. However, my weight loss stopped and my constipation was horrible. I was eating lots vegetables and salads, but it made no difference. I added psyllium husk and still no difference. After two months, my constipation problems resolved and I thought that my body had simply adjusted. I continued this way of eating afraid of gaining the 10 pounds back. I kept checking the strips to make sure I stayed in ketosis and was very strict with my diet. I ate \\"healthy\\" the way it indicated in all these books. I had lots of vegetables and a handful of berries each day, along with the regular meat, eggs and cheeses with no bread and no sugar. By February of 2012, I had a visible tumor on my neck. This scared the heck out of me, but I thought, \\"this can't be cancer....cancer feeds on sugar right?\\" Well, sure enough, it was a thyroid tumor. Now, these tumors are usually very treatable and not aggressive. However, after my surgery, I was told by my surgeon that it was very unusual to have this kind of tumor behave so aggressively and invade so many lymph nodes around my neck.<br /><br />Can you imagine how confused I was? Here I'd been on this no sugar, no carb diet for the last 6 months and yet.....a tumor had grown rapidly and aggressively in my neck.<br /><br />After researching for many many months as to how this could be, I came across some articles that say that ketones fuel tumors. I had a huge 'Aha' moment. This is another alternative idea as to why diabetics get more cancer. Is not that the sugar is feeding the cancer, but that the KETONES are feeding the tumor and it makes them more aggressive. I also kept coming across the Warburg Effect of cancer, but more importantly, came acrossthe REVERSE Warburg Effect which indicates that the cancer tumors have other ways of feeding themselves besides glucose. I also saw some reports or contra-arguments about that, but to me, I need no further proof. SUGAR DID NOT feed my tumor, but the ketones sure did. It also showed me that going into ketosis will NOT get rid of cancer tumors as I've seen over and over in many internet pages.<br /><br />I just want to warn people to please, please, please, take care of your health and not go into ketosis. Yes, I agree that lesser carbs is better, but some carbs are good. I found out about the relationship between IP6 and cancer. Where is IP6 found? In bran.....rice, corn, wheat. High intakes of IP6 have been strongly associated with less cancer.<br /><br />The main advice is to please read everything you can about an anti-cancer diet BEFORE you have cancer. Otherwise, you'll be like me, like a deer in the headlights....frozen and afraid.<br /><br />If you want to kill a cancer patient fast or if you want to grow a cancer, I am convinced, you just need to follow the advice in this book....Scary. Am paying the consequences and would hate to see more people be ignorant of these other factors.<br /><br />Stay healthy and informed. The worst thing to do is to be convinced that what you are doing is THE right way. Always stay curious and question everything, no matter how scientific it sounds. It applies to me too.

159514188X|1.0|SPOILER ALERT (although I prefer to think of it as reasons for you to not to even bother reading this terrible book.)<br /><br />I won't review the entire plot, since so many have done that in other reviews.  Rather, I'll just start off with the unrealistic and poorly thought-out details that screamed at me during my reading of this supposedly marvelous book.<br /><br />The day before Hannah commits suicide - likely several days after having started recording her twisted, sadistic set of audio tapes - she supposedly finally got around to considering how to actually go through with her suicide - by taking pills.  She first discarded other options, especially the idea of hanging herself, as she FINALLY acknowledged HER POOR PARENTS' potential future horror: \\"I could never get beyond the visual of someone finding me - swinging - inches from the floor.  I couldn't do that to Mom and Dad.\\"<br /><br />REALLY!!!!!!????  She just couldn't do THAT to mom and dad!  As if finding their only child dead from any other self-inflicted method would be oh-so-much-less terrible!  She then seemed to quickly decide to kill herself with an overdose of unidentified \\"pills\\" because she said it would be the least painful for her.  As if the prospect of enduring physical pain had ever appeared as something she was intent on avoiding in prior pages - a pesky detail the author chose to ignore.  She said, \\"So I've decided on the least painful way possible.  Pills.\\"  \\"But what kind of pills?  And how many?  I'm not sure.  And I don't have much time to figure it out because tomorrow...I'm going to do it.\\"  \\"I won't be around anymore...tomorrow.\\"<br /><br />\\"Tomorrow I'm getting up, I'm getting dressed, and I'm walking to the post office.  There, I'll mail a bunch of tapes to Justin Foley.  And after that, there's no turning back.\\"<br /><br />Such overplayed and poorly thought-out drama!  It was so improbable and seemed so thrown-together at the last minute!  Who did Hannah know who had the typeof pills capable of killing her for certain?  No such person was identified in the book previously.  Did she know that this dangerous drug-possessing person in her life left their medication unsecured, so anyone could just help themselves to the pills?  And, wouldn't they have noticed a that much of their presumably necessary medication was MISSING in time to raise an alarm?<br /><br />And, while I'm ripping the method of suicide chosen by this author; why Hannah (and, apparently Mr. Asher!) believed that taking a deadly overdose of \\"pills\\" would be not be painful is never even adressed!  For those suicidal teens who, after reading this book, may now actually believe Hannah's stupid assumption, general symptoms of a drug overdose may include some horribly painful consequences, including:<br /><br />Nausea<br />Vomiting<br />Abdominal cramps<br />Diarrhea<br />Dizziness<br />Loss of balance<br />Seizures<br />Headaches<br />Drowsiness<br />Confusion<br />Breathing difficulties<br />Internal bleeding<br />Hallucination<br />Visual disturbances<br />Coma - You might not succeed, and then you will have to live the rest of your life dealing with any damage you were able to inflict on your body.<br /><br />This critical detail of how to get whatever drug she planned to overdose with is just glossed over, as if the author had satisfied the minimum number of pages he needed to write and quickly wanted to wrap the story up, however untidily.  It was clear that the most important aspect of Hannah's suicide, in her mind, was the tapes - she spent a tremendous amount effort and creativity, and even seemed to enjoy planning every detail of the post-mortem method of tormenting those she left behind!  The method of her demise was treated as a relatively minor detail she breezily worked through after the tapes were pretty much finished and ready to mail off the following morning!  I suspect that such an important detail would be less of an after-thought for anyone truly planning their suicide.  I suspect it would take a lot more time and effort to obtain a sufficient quantity of pills capable of causing a deadly overdose without drawing the attention of anyone to her plan than the character Hannah gave herself.<br /><br />And, what about the kid in charge of her fail-safe?!  Are we really to believe that he was able to listen to all the tapes (fast-forwarding through them to make sure he wasn't on them) in less time than everyone else, so he KNEW she was planning to kill herself and that he would NOT have told an adult exactly what she had said on the tapes?  He was old enough to know better!  He would have called 911 and gotten everyone all worked up, even if she HAD already managed to take the pills.  If he had, it is very possible she would have been found in time to prevent her death.  It is just impossible that he would instead keep quiet, and then tail all the people who were sent the first set of tapes to ensure they kept mailing them on to the next person.  Why in the world would he do THAT instead of moving heaven and earth to try to make sure she was found before it was too late?  The only reasonable answer is that he would not do that.<br /><br />And, what about the guidance counselor?  Given the conversation he had just had with their daughter, a school counselor would have contacted the parents after the subject of suicide was so clearly a central topic in his conversation with their child!  If not, he would have to be a complete moron, who would very shortly thereafter lose his job for negligence!  (Conversations in \\"confidence\\" between a school employee and a minor are NOT iron-clad, especially regarding the topic of possible suicide!  Counselors touch base with parents for much less serious issues!)<br /><br />The thing that gets me the most, however, is Hannah's almost complete disregard of or concern for her parents.  We are supposed to believe that Hannah, who seemed really to have very little against her parents the few times that they were mentioned in the book, and who for a moment couldn't tolerate the sad thought of them finding her twisting dead at the end of a rope, didn't give THEM a chance to help her NOT kill herself?  Why the hell not?!  Why didn't they get a tape?  Why are they left wondering what they did wrong or what they missed, for the rest of their miserable lives?<br /><br />The reader is asked to believe they are SO out of touch with their daughter, due to distractions with their financial situation and struggling business, that they don't notice something is terribly wrong in the life of their only child!  Early in the book, Hannah confessed that her mom asked her about a boy who had called her at home - something a mother who notices and cares about what is going on in her pretty, young daughter's life would do!  I find it hard to believe that the otherwise responsible and intelligent-seeming parents of a teen going through all Hannah endured would be so completely oblivious.<br /><br />I can't understand why so many people proclaim this an important book regarding the topic of suicide!  The story is chock full of holes; there are so many places where responsible people would have interfered with Hannah's path to suicide.  I think this book is potentially dangerous, since it is such a popular read with teens right now.  It could just as likely inspire a struggling kid to decide to go ahead and commit suicide as it could possibly help another kid decide to ask for help in more realistic ways than the Hannah in this book did.<br /><br />The reason I happened to read this book is because my daughter snuck it into a small stack of paperbacks when we were last at a local bookstore, and I bought it without noticing.  When we got home, I told her I was not happy that she didn't ask me if she could have this book, and told her it was not ok to sneak purchases past me.  Rather than return it, I decided to read it first, figuring that if she wanted to read it, she'd just borrow it from a friend, so I might as well preview it.  After I finished it, I asked her to please not read it, and told her I thought it was a very poorly written book.  I also said I hoped she'd consider my opinion and read it with a very critical eye if she chose to read it anyway.  At best, it is a cheap tear-jerker.  In my opinion, it is not even close to an \\"important\\" book.  The topic of suicide is too serious to be so poorly researched and represented.

0385349831|4.0|It is the year 1099 and Crusaders have surrounded Jerusalem with the threat of invading the city. The townsfolk have gathered in the middle of the city and are receiving counsel from a learned man, a Greek, referred to as the Copt throughout the book.<br /><br />Most of the Copt's teaching and his words are mixed and slightly altered lines from other scriptures and texts in the world, although no credits are given.<br /><br />The entire book consists of questions from the people of the town and Copt's answers to them, with each question and answer constituting a chapter. Questions cover many topics such as solitude, love, sex, fear of change, anxiety, duty, defeat etc., and each one can easily be considered a sermon.<br /><br />As such, the answers to the questions are profound, but the Copt comes out as someone with self-importance like some prophet, although I don't think any real prophet would take himself as seriously.<br /><br />This book, however, has its basis in philosophy and advice on how to live; therefore, it wouldn't be fair to ask from it the requirements for fiction.<br /><br />This type of Book is good for taking out quotes from it and reading each chapter to muse on the concepts as if reading a piece of scripture. The ideas inside are profound and they do encourage a higher understanding of living, but if you are expecting another novel from Coelho like that of the Chemist or The Witch of Portobello, you won't find it in this book.<br /><br />I, however, enjoyed it greatly and found myself agreeing with the insights in the book.

B0089LOG02|4.0|Note: I received this book for free through SheReads.org in exchange for a fair and honest review as part of a blogger network.<br />I'm pleased to share that I have joined the SheReads blogger network, thanks to Traveling With T, who I'm fairly certain secured my invitation after the last Bloggiesta. After reading my first book for the club, I am even more excited than I was going into it!<br />May's pick was Orphan Train by Christina Baker Kline, which weaves the stories of a troubled 17 year old foster kid, Molly, and Vivian, an elderly woman with a troubled past. The book hits with the ground running, and once I started reading I couldn't put it down and read until I feel asleep and picked it up again before I was out of bed the next morning.<br />Molly, who has gotten herself into a bit of trouble, is sentenced to 50 hours of community service or will be sent to a juvenile detention facility. With the help of her boyfriend, Jack, she secures a place to contribute her time - which turns out to be helping Vivian clean out her attic. Vivian, an orphan train survivor living alone in a massive Maine house on the ocean, has worked hard to leave her past behind her and to move forward. But the ghosts of those that played an important part in her life remain wither her and leaving the past behind proves difficult.<br /><br />The book bounces back and forth to the early 1930′s to 2011, but most of the story takes place during Vivian's childhood rather than Molly's. The writing styles reflect the decades, so Vivian's is a bit more formal than Molly's, but Molly's story is the glue that holds it together. The story begins with Vivian's placement on an orphan train in NYC and follows her through to adulthood in Minnesota, with most of the emphasis being placed on the first twenty-five years of her life. Rather than tossing the items from the attic in the trash, the two wayward souls take stroll down memory lane, with each box they uncover opening a door to Vivian's past,and forging a friendship between the two that no one else understands. Together, Vivian and Molly face the past and tackle the future.<br />By the end of the book, I had experienced an entire range of emotions that included horror, sorrow, glee, and satisfaction (some simultaneously). Not only is this book impossible to put down, but this actually happened in the United States (see video below). A forgotten chapter of history, this is the first time I have read a book that focuses on the orphan trains and the 200,000 children that were transported to homes across the midwest. Vivian's tale highlights the ups and downs of the trek, as well as some of the situations that children were placed in - both the lucky and unlucky ones. I will definitely be looking into some non-fiction books about the orphan trains and I fully expect to experience a wide range of emotions.<br />In sum, READ THIS BOOK. And if you click here and leave a comment, you'll be entered to win a copy for FREE!<br />Oh, and check out the other SheReads reviews!

0060555661|4.0|Graham writes with a simple and direct style, but makes profound statements on the perils of behavioral finance.  For example, he points out early on that the investor's worst enemy is likely to be himself.  Investors motivated by greed flock to the sexiest, high-risk equities because the potential returns are high.  One does not need to look further than the tech bubble and the proliferation of mortgage backed securities for evidence of emotion-oriented investing.  Graham dispels this notion by advocating for principle-based value investing and intelligent asset allocation between stocks and bonds.<br /><br />The distinction between investing and speculating is useful, especially for people without an academic or professional background in finance.  In Graham's words: \\"An investment operation is one which, upon thorough analysis promises safety of principal and an adequate return.  Operations not meeting these requirements are speculative.\\" He then divides investors into two categories- defensive and aggressive- that profile how much time and risk an individual is willing and able to input into investing.  The book is principally a guide to value investing, or selecting stocks that you believe the market has overpriced.<br /><br />It's easy to understand the intention behind adding Jason Zweig's commentary--the idea was to provide contemporary context for an outdated book.  However, Zweig's writing detracts from the original message more than it adds value.  Graham's insights are clear enough to be understood and applied to modern financial markets by any reasonable, sensible person.  From a reader's perspective, it would have been convenient for Zweig's comments to be published in a separate book or after Graham's writing.  Transitioning between two vastly different writing styles and time periods was a challenge to the extent it prevented a 5 star rating.<br /><br />After reading other reviewers wish they would have known about the Intelligent Investor years ago,I recommend buying the text for a high school and college aged students.  If the material is initially over their head, there are resources such as Investopedia and the Economist to provide clarification and explanation on specific terms and concepts.  Investing money at a young age is wise because compound interest magnifies your returns and the market trends upwards over time.  Investing money at a young age is foolish because you don't have the expertise or knowledge that a seasoned investor brings to the table.  That's why young people should read as much as they can before jumping into the markets.  At 20 years of age, I have taken a plethora of finance and accounting courses at a highly regarded business school and managed my IRA account and stock portfolio, but I recognize there is still much to learn.  The next challenge awaiting me is Graham's seminal book \\"Security Analysis\\".

B002KRFND0|5.0|The Sherlock Holmes stories by Sir Arthur Conan Doyle are delightfully written, and have great central characters; the brilliant, wonderfully eccentric Holmes, and his faithful companion, Watson.<br /><br />This volume is a complete, correctly arranged and beautiful book.  It is somewhat heavy, but not cumbersome.  The pages have a quality feel, though there are several type errors; however, they are not so prevalent as to justify removing a star from its rating.<br /><br />The vast majority of the short stories and all four novels are fun and believable, with the exception of perhaps the Greek Interpreter, and The Engineer's thumb.  The plots are compelling, as are the characters of Holmes and Watson; they balance each other out nicely.  I soon grew so fond of both that I  was completely heartbroken after reading The Final Problem; reading the Empty House was the perfect cure! My personal favorites are the Beryl Coronet, The Boscombe Valley Mystery, and The Mazarin Stone, and the Noble Bachelor.<br /><br />Here are summaries of some, in my opinion, the best stories:<br />(The Beryl Coronet) As security for the loan of a large sum, Mr. Holder, acting for the bank at which he works, receives a tremendously valuable beryl coronet. When that very night he finds his son handling it, he finds that three of the stones are missing! Crazed with worry, grief, and shame, he turns to Sherlock Holmes, in hope that he can return the missing section of the Beryl Coronet. Holmes must succeed where the police have failed, and restore the stones, or a devastating public scandal will ensue.<br /><br />(The Boscombe Valley Mystery) A  tragedy has occurred in Boscomb Valley. Mr. Charles McCarthy has been murdered! All evidence points to his son, James McCarthy, as being guilty. However, his neighbor's daughter insists that he is innocent, and requests that Sherlock Holmes, the famous detective, be summoned to investigate. Will James McCarthy be convicted? Or can Holmes find the evidence needed to prove his innocence?<br /><br />(The Noble Bachelor) Lord St. Simon, one of the most prestigious names in London, has become victim to terrible misfortune. One the very day of his wedding, after the ceremony and during the breakfast, his bride mysteriously disappeared. Lestrade, as well as Sherlock Holmes, has taken on the case, though he can make nothing of it. While in conversation with him, Holmes declares that \\"Lady St. Simon is a myth. There is not, and there never ha been any such person.\\" What can he mean?  Read it and find out.<br /><br />These stories, in my mind, are perfect; they never get boring, and are infinitely re-readable.  Enjoy!<br /><br />(If after reading these, you desire to see them as movies, [[ASIN:B000060MVK The Adventures of Sherlock Holmes (Boxed Set Collection)]] and [[ASIN:B00009WVO2 The Return of Sherlock Holmes Collection]] stay closest to the original stories.)

B009NY43NY|4.0|WARNING******SPOILERS<br /><br />I am a little upset with all the negativity and hostility surrounding this book. The idea that Sookie lost her voice and didn't stay true to character is preposterous.  Of course she changed some after 13 books, countless beatings and betrayals, love and loss, deaths, births, weddings, and life-altering revelations. She's killed people, and that'll change anyone who's not a sociopath.<br /><br />I can somewhat understand the outrage over the way things with Eric were handled. It was rushed. It was abrupt. It was disappointing to see one of my favorite characters of all time get such a horrible ending. However, as much as we fans all loved him, he is NOT our character. I also thought there were too many plotholes. This set was a lot tamer that what people got used to later in their career.  With production by Ron Nevison (Led Zeppelin, UFO, Bad Company, Ted Nugent etc.) and Phil, this was a change of pace from \\"Vagabounds\\".  The album starts off with the bouncy breezy charmer \\"She Knows\\", the slow blues of \\"Night Life\\", some sweet strings on this one.  The rocker \\"It's Only Money\\", covered by Concrete Blonde on 1988's \\"Free\\", album, Brian Downey's drums are sharp here.  The classic \\"Still In Love With You\\", with Frankie Miller joining Phil on vocals, great guitar by Brian and Scott.  With keyboard work by John Russell and some strings on \\"Frankie Carroll\\", very Beatles sounding.  The funky \\"Showdown\\", with Phil's bass just right, GREAT stuff!  The swirling guitars of \\"Banshee\\".  \\"Philomena\\", Phil wrote this song for his mother, a folk rock sound here, nice guitar break by Brian.  \\"Sha-La-La\\", is a souped upped raver, great guitar and bass, fun song.  And the album ends with the pretty ballad \\"Dear Heart\\", with some tasty guitar and strings.  Some of the band had a problem with this record, but i find it very soothing in their catalog.  The bonus material you get,  6 BBC session tracks, 3 fantastic demo performances from Gary Moore on \\"Showdown\\", \\"It's Only Money\\" and \\"Still In Love With You\\".  Thin Lizzy fans should listen to this album.

B009NY43NY|4.0|Summary: Sookie Stackhouse used her wish to save Sam Merlotte's life. Unhappy with Sookie's decision, Eric stormed out, now at a loss for how to keep him and her together. When Sookie's is accused of murder, things get more and more confusing for the telepath. Will she ever live happily ever after?<br /><br />Review: I will admit I have a different perspective from other readers. For one, I dislike the show. I struggled through season 1 and then stopped after the first two episodes of season 2. Also, I read more in this genre than just the Sookie Stackhouse books, such as the Hollows, Kate Daniels, and Dresden Files. Finally, I picked up these books recently, as in Friday, and read all 13 books and the short stories in a matter of 5 days.<br /><br />I enjoyed the final installment of the series. The world Harris built is charming, albeit a bit predictable. I like it when vampires are not perfect creatures. Harris' vamps are as pretty as they are selfish. I like the extra spin she put on shape shifters, with the whole \\"pure shape shifters\\". Fairys were typical fae, and we had witches. But although similar to Urban Fantasy, the southern nature of the characters is lovely in its own way.<br /><br />I enjoyed the fact that Ms. Harris kept her characters consistent. Everyone behaved according to his or her nature. The Vampires put themselves first, Shape shifters represented their animals. Sookie herself stayed true to her southern nature. Yet the characters grew. Bill went from first love to high betrayer to a good friend. He learned to be what Sookie needed him to be at the end. Tara realized money wasn't everything and balanced her life. Sookie went from pushover to a woman who knows what she wants. In the end, the characters grew into themselves.<br /><br />The plot of the book was better than the last 3 books, but it wasn't as good as the first half of the books. I liked that this book had a clear beginning middle and end, and I was missing that in some of the laterbooks. I enjoyed the clear and present mystery. I also appreciated the end of the overall plot of the whole series. From the beginning, it was clear to me that Sookie's life was going to boil down to two choices: Supernatural life or Home. In many ways, it represented to me big city life vs. small town life. And for Sookie, she always wanted to \\"come home\\", especially since All Together Dead. Harris neatly tied up the loose ends, and although it was predictable, I liked the presentation.<br /><br />I do think Harris could have tightened up the story in many places. The third person POV didn't work for me (to the point where I almost gave the book 3 stars instead of 4). I think she should have left out those POVs and covered Sookie's self discovery more. I think she could have included more information regarding Sookie's choices and hometown love, to make her point. I think this message got lost around the time Sookie and Eric got serious. It was left out (or glossed over) in the last few books, too. I think long term planning would have really helped out with the end of this series.<br /><br />Of course, this review has to include the relationship aspect. I always knew it was going to be Sam. All those intimate talks and those kisses had to go somewhere. More importantly, Sookie's journey is about coming home and facing the sun. She was never satisfied with vampires and their lifestyle, and they would never be able to compromise to her. At the end of \\"All together Dead\\", I knew she was going to end up at least with no vampires. But as I said before, tighter writing would have given readers a clearer picture to this romance. I appreciated that we were left with room to speculate, with her and Sam taking it a slow. This bit of realism was refreshing.<br /><br />With all the media surrounding this book, I was pleasantly surprised with the ending Ms. Harris supplied us. I am glad I gave this series a shot.<br /><br />Sex: Nice steamy, but realistically romantic sex scene.<br />Violence: Shootings, death, kidnappings<br /><br />Rating: 4 out of 5 stars<br />Series Rating: 3 out of 5 stars<br /><br />Quote: \\"`Maybe' is such a bad word,\\" I said. \\"You can `maybe' yourself back to Adam and Eve and the serpent.\\"<br /><br />Next Book: This was the last of the series, but [[ASIN:B00BDQ3B4W After Dead: What Came Next in the World of Sookie Stackhouse]], is a CODA of the characters and what happened to them after the series ended. It comes out 10/29/2013

B001DM1VXE|4.0|This is a stunning movie by Darren Aronofsky with a unique vision, but it is not for everyone. Shot in sharp, grainy, bold black and white, it is the story of an obsessed mathematics whiz named Max who might be more than slightly mad: he triple locks himself in his apartment, avoids people like they're infectious and works continually upon a complex self-made computer named EUCLID in order to unlock secret patterns in the universe. He knows this order exists, though his only real friend and mentor Sol Robeson (Mark Margolis) insists it doesn't. Max, however, sees it all around him: in the newspaper, in art, in the swirls of cream in his coffee -- the only problem is he isn't sure what it will look like when it comes.  He certainly doesn't think it will look like this long tangle of numbers his computer spits out, even one 216 numbers long.<br /><br />An unfortunate sign he's on to something, however, is that people will not leave him alone. They're following him, constantly, and the tension is driving him mad...his migraines seem to slice into his brain. If he's lucky, he just blacks out after a few agonizing minutes. But lately he's been seeing things: dark visions of bleeding men and dark, angry people, leering faces, and he's also hearing horrible, discordant noises that batter him from within. Those trying to cling to him believe he is onto something - while some market traders have indeed been tracking him in the hope that Max will make them rich, some Kabballistic Jews are on his tail in their belief he may just be able to help them discover the true name of God...a 216-number sequence.<br /><br />It bears mentioning that I have seen, and admired, two previous Aronofsky movies - [[ASIN:B00274EYKY The Wrestler]] and [[ASIN:B004U8VWLE Black Swan]] - which I thought were terrific.  Some years earlier I had quit watching one - [[ASIN:B000RZAENG The Fountain]] - which I felt was interminably dull. Yet I found PI to be enthralling; I began it an hour before midnight after a long day and told myself I would just get a taste of it and turn it off when I was tired - yet the first moment I even thought of what time it was occurred about seven minutes before the credits rolled. I had been thatenthralled.<br /><br />What is accomplished most successfully here is Sean Gullette's portrayal of Max with all his \\"frayed edges,\\" a man whose bright, facile mind fuels his pyrotechnic manias, and it is indicative of Aronofsky's skilled direction that we often feel we are seeing the world through his eyes. This presents, of course, its own problem: as anyone who has taken a literature course knows, Crazy Man = Unreliable Narrator. But we have enough to go on here to realize that it doesn't particularly matter if EVERYTHING Max is relating to us via his narration is true; the story as I have related it to you is enough to carry the day. Max is brilliant; his computer program is inspired, and he is an untapped source others want to use to their own ends.<br /><br />Another note: cinematographer Matthew Libatique deserves a great deal of credit...the stark contrasts and almost overdeveloped film are symbolic of his rigid view and his anger at those in his way, as well as the fact that for him, the \\"answers\\" are, indeed, black and white...clear...like math. Likewise, credit should also go to Clint Mansell, who put together the musical score - it's full of strident, chaotic electronic music, IDM and hip hop that thump and jar and epitomize the jangling paranoia and rage and tension evident continually.<br /><br />Of importance, too, is the fact that the ending is satisfying. Aronofsky settles the issue, and the movie will present a resolution. Like Max, we the audience get to come to a place where there is an understanding.<br /><br />(A NOTE: if you give a negative review, please leave a comment to help me do better next time.)after a long day and told myself I would just get a taste of it and turn it off when I was tired - yet the first moment I even thought of what time it was occurred about seven minutes before the credits rolled. B002CRJWXA|5.0|This box contains Brendel's first Mozart cycle, with Marriner and the Academy, expertly remastered and offered in the bargain-basement price range. As such it is excellent value, despite skimpy notes (in German).  Brendel's way with Mozart is well-known and often celebrated.  Not for him the expressive nuances of Perahia, Uchida or Barenboim.  Brendel's Mozart is vigorous, plainspoken and, at times, a bit prosaic.  In compensation, however, the listener gets a non-interventionist, but always stylish, reading of Mozart as near as possible to authentic performance practice as can be conveyed on a modern piano, with a modern-instrument chamber orchestra of appropriate size.  If that approach to these evergreen works appeals to you, then this set could function as a a fine \\"starter collection\\" of the Mozart Concertos (minus the four from KV 107 and those numbered 1-4, all of which derive from works of other composers, though they are arranged as concertos by Mozart himself). The set does include the two- and three-piano Concertos, nos. 10 & 7 respectively, though the latter is given in a very effective arrangement for two pianos.  Both are played with zest and imagination by Brendel and Imogen Cooper.<br /><br />Among the highlights of Brendel's cycle, I would cite the following:  6, 8, 9, 13, 14, 18, 19, 22, 23, 25, and 27. All of these remain free of the didacticism that could sometimes afflict Brendel's playing in the 1970's and 1980's. Few versions of no. 9 convey it's bold inventiveness as effectively as Brendel/Marriner, and I would cite that performance as the high-point of Brendel's cycle for this listener.  Nos. 20 and 24 are appropriately dramatic in a Beethovenian way, though occasionally the brittleness of Brendel's approach--and his tone--prove irksome.  That brittleness is also in evidence in no. 5, which is altogether too aggressive, and intermittently in nos. 15, 21 and 26 as well.  But I am not disposed to overemphasize these liabilities, for Brendel proves throughout this set to be a more than reliable guide to repertoire he obviously knows intimately and loves enthusiastically.  That enthusiasm ultimately wins the day, even in certain works where one might wish for a more gracious, lyrical approach.  For listeners who might find Perahia too delicate, Barenboim too personal, Ashkenazy too robust, and Uchida too introverted (among the complete cycles with which I am familiar), Brendel's collaboration with Marriner and the Academy at the heght of their collective powers might be your best bet.  Moreover, having a first-rate exponent of 18th-Century music at the helm assures that the orchestral contribution has the expert leadership it sometimes lacks in pianist-directed versions of these works.<br /><br />Altogether, a successful venture, strongly recommended, particularly in this price range.  Of course, every classical music lover and pianophile should at least consider obtaining certain Mozart concerto recordings that have deservedly acquired classic status--such as Edwin Fischer in many of the late concertos; Haskil in nos. 9, 13, 29 and 24; Serkin/Szell in 19 & 20; Casadesus/Szell in 21-24, 26-27; Curzon in 20, 23, 24, and 27; Gilels in 27; Kempff in 8, 22, 23, 24 and 27; and Bernstein (as soloist and conductor) in 15.  I would also strongly recommend Brendel's earlier recordings for Vox (particularly 25, 27 and the two-piano Concerto with Klien) and for Vanguard (9 & 14 with Janigro/Zagreb). Haebler's distinctive--if rather \\"dresden-china-ish\\" cycle with Rowiki for Phillips is regrettably long-gone, as is (I believe) Kraus's less consistently successful cycle with Simon and a pick-up Viennese Orchestra.  If some or all of those two cycles were reissued, that would be a benefaction for collectors.  Kraus's splendid versions of 12 & 18 with Monteux and the BSO is currently available for direct order from Haydn House, in good transfers (don't hesitate if you are an admirer of the pianist).oves throughout this set to be a more than reliable guide to repertoire he obviously knows intimately and loves enthusiastically. B009AMAJVO|4.0|\\"Love can heal the most damaged heart\\", Dead Man Down begins with a character saying these words, so it isn't too hard to guess that this will be the theme found within it. Which it is, and if the move had stayed with just that it would have been one of the best movies in a god long while, but it tragically unspools into an explicably silly action thriller by it's far too routine ending, however given the 85% or 90%  of the film that unfolds before it, I can with certainty say that Dead Man Down is quite a good film, one that threatens constantly to reach the level of greatness. However, it falls victim to current movie trends.<br /> Starring the always unswerving Colin Farrell as Victor, a small time mob enforcer harboring some potentially dangerous secrets and inner demons. He enters into a relationship with a neighbor named Beatrice (Noami Rapace) who also has a darkness of her own. Beatrice witnessed a killing carried out by Victor and even recorded it. Some time ago Beatrice was partially disfigured in a car wreck, know tormented by neighborhood kids who scrawl \\"monster\\" on her apartment door. Beatrice wants Victor to kill the man, who while drunk and driving, was responsible for the accident.<br /> This is the set up for a dark and fascinating neo-noir. In many ways Dead Man Down is a kin to the amazing Drive, a gutsy and fastidious attempt at telling an existential drama that is closer in tone and style to the classics of the genre than we have been accustomed to in recent years.<br /> Dead Man Down marks the first English language film from Niels Arden Opley, the Swedish director behiend the original Swedish language the Girl with the Dragoon Tattoo. Here he's reteamed with his lovely and exciting leading lady Rapace. Opley shows a great visual flair and is more than capable of telling a good or better story in the always fascinating world of noir.<br /> Working from a solid, but unexceptional script by J.H. Wyman. Dead Man Down comes with the promise of being a standard all-too familiar revenge sage, but for the most part it is so much more than that. A complicated character driven love story set to a backdrop of appropriate doom and gloom, it only surrenders to it's marketing campaign with a highly improbable guns-a-blazing ending.<br /> A lot of the credit rests on the shoulders of the movie's fine cast. Farrell and Rapace sell there characters to us so convincingly that we fail to question some the more obvious absurdities in it. The two talented and charismatic leads oodles with chemistry and Dead Man Down really comes to it's dark and convincing life when these two are on screen.<br /> Also very good is the underappreciated actor Dominic Cooper, who if more people had seen his excellent work in the Devil`s Double, I am convinced he would be a major star now and a leading man. Terence Howard also manages to be quite good, despite playing a rather one dimensional cartoonish villain. Adding to the cast are some nice familiar faces that we haven't seen criminally in a long awhile, Isabelle Huppert, F. Murray Abraham and Armand Assante.<br /> Some nice technical work is on clear display as well, Dead Man Down is nicely photographed by stylish action cinematographer Paul Cameron. The action scenes are agreeably edited by the duo of Timothy A. Good and Frederic Thoraval. An interesting looking production designed from Niels Sejer helps establish the noir atmosphere.<br /> All was in place for Dead Man Down to be one of the best movies of the year and a glorious American debut for the newly arrived director Opley, sadly it just wasn't meant to be and is never allowed to jell. Dead Man Down begins to crumple in it's last fifteen or twenty minutes as it becomes ever more ludicrously contrived and ends in a preposterous showdown that looks like it was ripped from a completely different and less imaginative movie.<br /> The over the top action conclusion should not have come as much of a surprise given that the film is brought us by the good people at WWE films, the movie branch of the world wrestling  entertainment.. It just seems so disappointing that it does, and because so much of the movie was just on the verge of excellent that it's unsatisfactory conclusion catches us off guard. It is also plainly unnecessary.<br /> I was willing to give WWE films the benefit of the doubt that they could be able to make a smart and complicated thriller without resorting to the lower standards they are famous for. However, this absurd and forced finale seems as though it would be more at home in one of their direct-to-DVD sequels to the Marine.<br /> Dead Man Down ultimately will leave any self respecting viewer unhappy and discouraged, maybe even angry, with just how close it came to being something truly special. A real tragic missed opportunity, but taking into account all the good that is here, this is still a first class entertainment and a worthy entry in the cannon of contemporary crime thrillers

B0000VCZK2|5.0|These photographs are from an ongoing body of work entitled Kokomo, which centers around my immediate family and hometown, a coastal suburb in the northeast.  Incorporating constructed dioramas, digital manipulation, appropriated family photographs and traditional photography, the work forgoes a diaristic approach to the subject matter, and instead builds an alternate reality over my own biography, abstracting the specifics of a personal narrative into an elusive, alien world.<br /><br />There is no distinction between landscape, portrait, or still life in the work.  The photographs phase in and out of genre, referencing notions of spectacle, queerness, and horror, while continually deconstructing and reconfiguring the roles of the subjects and the world they inhabit.  Linear time is collapsed throughout the photographs, blurring the boundaries between past, present, and future.<br /><br />Though an immensely personal body of work, specific details are rendered insignificant by new forms that emerge.  What remains are photographs of a town ruined by a storm, and a family getting old.

030788743X|1.0|This is a book with a very promising premise that turns out to be nothing other than a gimmick, the publisher identifying a niche marketing segment and pushing something on them. There's no geek culture, there's just a geek market. The premise, the hunt through a massive on-line multiplayer game for riches, with all the clues and methods built around 1980s TV/music/video games trivia, is a concept that never rises to an idea. Cline demonstrates that he thinks dropping names of bands and TV shows makes for culture, exemplifying the geek-hipster idea of enthusiasm for collecting shiny things without any actual interest or pleasure in them.<br /><br />An example is the avatar name that the protagonist uses, Parzival, which he, as the narrator, explains come out of his knowledge of [[ASIN:B002PJL85K Excalibur]], John Boorman's excellent 1980s retelling of Malory. But using the name doesn't signify culture, it signifies hipsterism. The parallel between the search for the keys in the on-line game and the hunt for the Holy Grail is nothing more than that of a search for objects, there is no cultural equivalent between the riches that the key brings and the renewal and transformation of a civilization from paganism to Christianity that the Grail brings. \\"Excalibur\\" retells that story and weaves in centuries of western art that straddles this religious transformation, while \\"Ready Player One\\" borrows the name. If you want cultural ideas, you have to think them and then convey them, and Cline does neither.<br /><br />What he does do, interminably, is lecture: he explains every single thing that pops up, constantly tells rather than shows. He writes in an eager style that lacks any particular qualities and the book comes off as written by a slightly precocious adolescent boy. It's not at an adult level, nor is it written with the skill and understanding that a good adult writer brings to a YA novel. The dull writing and excessive telling mean that \\"Ready Player One\\" is about twice as long as it should have been -- there are some attempts at suspense, but Cline is unable to actually produce any tension or surprises, and it's a tiresome and dull read. Ignore the hype and the hipster affirmations from people too young to remember the 1980s, this is a terrible book.wice as long as it should have been -- there are some attempts at suspense, but Cline is unable to actually produce any tension or surprises, and it's a tiresome and dull read.
